Refutation of Mu'tazili Division of Act and Ugliness
Refutation of Mu'tazili division of act creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazili view that the servant creates the act and God creates its ugliness is self-refuting and leads to shirk.

Core Idea
Dividing creation of an act between God and servant leads to shirk and contradiction.

This passage presents a key Mu'tazili objection from al-Ka'bi and a refutation. The objection attempts to preserve human freedom by assigning the creation of the act's essence to the servant and its attribute of ugliness to God. The refutation dismantles this position from multiple angles, showing it contradicts Mu'tazili own principles and implies shirk.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazili Objection
Read the objection carefully: the servant creates disbelief (essence), God creates its ugliness (attribute). This is al-Ka'bi's distinction between free and compelled.
The objection uses the concept that attributes of acts return to the acts themselves.
2
First Refutation: Aspects of the Act
The first point: this view necessitates affirming 'aspects of the act' (jihāt al-fi'l), which the Mu'tazila themselves reject.
3
Second Refutation: Admitting the Problem
The second point: it admits the entry of the decreed into the act, which the Mu'tazila consider their strongest objection.
4
Third Refutation: Shirk
The third point: it establishes shirk between God and the servant in the act, which is false.
5
Fourth Refutation: Foolishness
The fourth point: if God creates ugliness, He becomes the creator of ugliness, which would make Him foolish—impossible for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لما كانت صفات الأفعال راجعة إلى أعيانها فالكفر إذا فعل وهو قبيح فيتولى العبد إيجاده من حيث الكفر، ويتولى الله تعالى إيجاده من حيث القبح، وكذا هذا في المشى والإيذاء والإيلام، وهذا معنى ما ذكر الكعبي أن العبد مختار من فعله مضطر في تألمه، والمختار عنده من تولى إيجاد الكفر، والمضطر عنده من تولى غيره إيجاد صفة فيه لا قدرة له عليها.
قيل : هذا السؤال على أصلكم فاسد من وجوه:
أحدها: أنه يوجب القول بجهات الفعل وهو عندكم باطل.
والآخر : اعتراف بما جعلتموه من أقوى الشبهات لكم في المسألة وهو دخول مقدور الفعل والقول بذلك باطل.
والثالث أن هذا عندكم إثبات الشرك بين الله تعالى وبين العبد في هذا الفعل، والقول بذلك باطل.
والرابع: أن موجد القبح الراجع إلى ذات القبيح كان موجدًا للقبيح، وعندكم موجد القبيح سفيه والله تعالى يتعالى عن فعل السفه بلا خلاف بيننا وبينكم وإن كان عندكم موصوفا بالقدرة عليه.
If they say: Since the attributes of actions return to their essences, so disbelief when done is ugly, so the servant undertakes its creation in terms of disbelief, and God undertakes its creation in terms of ugliness, and likewise in walking, harming, and causing pain. This is what al-Ka'bi mentioned: that the servant is free in his act but compelled in his pain; the free according to him is the one who undertakes the creation of disbelief, and the compelled is the one for whom another creates an attribute in him over which he has no power. It is said: This question according to your principle is invalid in several respects: One: It necessitates the assertion of aspects of the act (jihāt al-fi'l), which according to you is false. Another: It is an admission of what you have made as your strongest objection in the issue, namely the entry of the decreed [into the act], and asserting that is false. Third: This according to you is establishing shirk between God and the servant in this act, and asserting that is false. Fourth: The one who creates ugliness that returns to the essence of the ugly is the creator of the ugly, and according to you the creator of the ugly is foolish, and God is exalted above committing foolishness without dispute between us and you, even if He is described according to you as having power over it.
Source pages p. 880

Commentary
This passage presents a Mu'tazili objection attributed to al-Ka'bi and then refutes it with four points. The objection claims that because attributes of actions return to the acts themselves, the servant creates the act in its specific nature (e.g., disbelief), while God creates its attribute of ugliness. Al-Ka'bi's view distinguishes between the servant's freedom in creating the act and his compulsion in experiencing pain. The refutation first points out that this view necessitates affirming 'aspects of the act' (jihāt al-fi'l), which the Mu'tazila themselves reject. Second, it admits the problematic concept of the decreed entering the act, which the Mu'tazila consider their strongest objection. Third, it implies shirk (associating partners with God) by making both God and the servant creators of the same act. Fourth, if God creates ugliness, He becomes the creator of ugliness, which would make Him foolish—something impossible for God according to both parties, even though the Mu'tazila claim He has power over it.

Key Terms
صفات الأفعال
جهات الفعل
إيجاد
قبح
شرك
سفيه
كعبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That it is reasonable to divide the creation of an act between God (creating ugliness) and the servant (creating the act itself).
Correction: This leads to shirk, contradicts Mu'tazili own principles (jihāt al-fi'l), and makes God the creator of ugliness, which implies foolishness.

Checkpoint
What is the third reason the author gives for the invalidity of the Mu'tazili objection?
A) It necessitates aspects of the act
B) It admits the strongest objection of the opponents
C) It establishes shirk between God and the servant
D) It makes God the creator of ugliness

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili attempt to divide creation of the act between servant and God is self-refuting, as it violates their own principles and implies shirk and divine imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your heart, do you ever subtly divide the credit for your good deeds to God and the blame for your sins to yourself in a way that compromises tawheed? How does this lesson's warning against shirk in act creation expose that deeper tendency? What will you do today to repent of any such mental division?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending God's perfection by distancing Him from ugliness, yet their reasoning led to attributing foolishness to Him. Are there areas in your own understanding of God where you, in trying to protect His honor, actually fall into a similar trap of flawed logic? What step will you take to submit to the divine revelation even when it challenges your reasoning?
?
If God alone is the Creator of all actions, including your sins, how does that truth change the way you view your failures? Do you still find yourself blaming circumstances or others rather than turning to God in repentance and gratitude? What specific sin will you confess today, acknowledging that it occurred only by His will and decree?

The Maturidi Doctrine of Kasb and the Resolution of the Dilemma
Author explains Maturidi doctrine of acquisition and answers objection on God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi doctrine of kasb (acquisition) and how it resolves the logical dilemma of compulsion versus disability in divine power.

Core Idea
God's custom links act creation to the servant's intention, preserving both divine power and human responsibility.

The Maturidi school carefully navigates between absolute divine power and human free will through the concept of kasb. In this passage, the author defends the Maturidi position against an objection that would seemingly force either compulsion or a limit on God's power. You will see how the argument hinges on the nature of elective acts and divine custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: تخليق (creation), اكتساب (acquisition), استبداد (exclusive power), عادة (custom).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Maturidi position
Locate the statement that God has exclusive power over every act, but He customarily creates the act only when the servant intends to acquire it. This is the core of kasb.
3
Understand the hypothetical dilemma
The objection presents a scenario: if God wills an act for a servant who intends to refrain, either the act occurs (leading to compulsion) or it doesn't (implying God's inability). Follow the logic carefully.
This is a classic objection against the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions.
4
Analyze the Maturidi response
The author argues that the scenario is impossible because an elective act requires the servant's own power and will; God's will for the act depends on the servant's will for his power. Thus, the dilemma does not arise.
Key insight: the servant's intention to refrain is incompatible with God's will for the act in the Maturidi framework.
5
Reflect on the role of divine custom
Note how the Maturidi appeal to God's custom (ʿāda) to link creation to the servant's intention, preserving both divine sovereignty and human accountability.
This distinguishes Maturidism from both Jabriyya (pure compulsion) and Qadariyya (absolute free will).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يلزمنا هذا في التخليق والانتساب على ما قررنا في شبهة الخصوم، لأن كل فعل في ذاته يوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على الانفراد والاستبداد، والعبد لا يوصف بذلك، إلا أن الله تعالى أجرى العادة أنه يخلق كل فعل قصد العبد اكتسابه. ونفرض الكلام في حركة معينة فنقول : هي قبيل وجودها علمها الله تعالى قدرة إيجادها لو أراد إيجادها على سبيل الاستبداد، والعبد لا يقدر على اكتسابها ومباشرتها بدون إيجاد الله تعالى.
ولا يقال لو أراد الله إيجادها فعلاً للعبد مع امتناع العبد عن ذلك هل يحصل ذلك؟ فإن حصل فقد لزم القول باضطرار العبد إذ الفعل الضروري هو الذي يوجد والذي قصد العبد الامتناع عنه، وإن لم يحصل فقد لزم القول بتعجيز الباري جل وعلا، لأنا نقول : لا يتصور عندنا أن يريدها فعلاً للعبد إلا وأن يريد العبد وجود قدرته عليها لاستحالة وجود الفعل الاختياري بلا قدرة ولا يتصور وجود القدرة بدون الفعل فيستحيل إرادة الله إياها أن تكون فعلاً للعبد مع إرادة العبد الامتناع
And this does not follow for us in creation and attribution as we established in the objection of the opponents, because every act in itself is described by God having power over it alone and exclusively, and the servant is not described with that, except that God has made it customary that He creates every act that the servant intends to acquire. And we hypothesize regarding a specific movement and say: Before its existence, God is described with the power to create it if He wanted to create it exclusively, and the servant is not able to acquire and perform it without God's creation. And it is not said: If God wanted to create it as an act of the servant while the servant refrains, would that happen? If it does happen, then it necessitates the affirmation of compulsion of the servant, since the compelled act is that which occurs even though the servant intended to refrain from it. And if it does not happen, then it necessitates the affirmation of disabling the Exalted. For we say: It is inconceivable in our view that He would want it as an act of the servant unless the servant wants the existence of his power over it, because the existence of an elective act without power is impossible, and power is not conceived without the act, so it is impossible for God's will for it to be an act of the servant while the servant intends to refrain.
Source pages p. 881

Commentary
This passage clarifies the Maturidi doctrine of kasb (acquisition) and answers a potential objection. The author first states that the previous dilemma does not apply to the Maturidi view because they hold that God has absolute power over every act exclusively, while the servant has no independent power. However, God has established a custom (ʿāda) that He creates the act only when the servant intends to acquire it. Using the example of a specific movement, the author explains that before its existence, God has the power to create it independently, but the servant cannot acquire or perform it without God's creation. Then the author addresses the objection: if God wanted to create an act for a servant who intends to refrain, would it occur? If it occurs, the servant is compelled (since the act happens despite his intention to refrain). If it does not occur, God is seemingly disabled. The Maturidi response is that it is inconceivable for God to will an act for a servant without the servant also willing his own power for it, because an elective act requires power, and power is inseparable from the act. Thus, the scenario where the servant intends to refrain is impossible in the first place, so the dilemma is resolved.

Key Terms
تخليق
انتساب
عادة
اكتساب
قدرة
استبداد
إرادة
اضطرار
تعجيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has absolute power, He could create an act for a disbelieving servant regardless of the servant's will, making the servant compelled.
Correction: The Maturidi view holds that God's custom links creation to the servant's intention; an elective act requires the servant's own power and will, so the scenario is impossible by definition.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi view, why can God not create an act for a servant who intends to refrain?
A) Because God cannot override human will
B) Because the servant's power is independent
C) Because an elective act requires the servant's own power and will, and God's custom links creation to the servant's intention
D) Because God is unable to create in that case

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi doctrine of kasb resolves the dilemma of compulsion vs. disability by asserting that divine custom links act creation to the servant's intention, making the problematic scenario impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself committing a sin, do you ever feel a sense of compulsion, as if you had no choice? How does the Maturidi teaching that God creates the act only when you intend it confront that feeling? What intention are you actually choosing in that moment, and are you willing to own it as your acquisition before God?
?
The passage states that it is impossible for God to will an act as your act if you intend to refrain. Think of a habit you have tried to break but returned to. In light of this teaching, can you honestly say you intended to refrain? Or did your heart still desire it? How does this doctrine force you to take full responsibility for your intentions rather than blaming God's decree?
?
Given that God's custom links the creation of good deeds to your sincere intention, what does this imply about your prayer and worship? Do you rush through them without checking your intention? How will you change your approach to ensure that your intention is truly directed towards God's pleasure, so that He creates the act as your acquisition?

The Dilemma of Mu'tazila on Divine Compulsion or Free Will
Author presents a dilemma against Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma the author poses to the Mu'tazila regarding God's role in creating the evil quality of an act.

Core Idea
If God must create evil, either He is free or compelled—both impossible.

The Mu'tazila claim that the servant creates the act as an act while God creates its evil quality. The author presents a devastating dilemma: either God is free to create the evil or He is compelled. Each horn leads to an impossibility, refuting their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure of the dilemma.
This passage contains the core refutation.
2
Identify the two options
The author presents two possibilities for God's role in creating evil. Find them in the text and note the outcome of each.
One leads to impossibility, the other to kufr.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the Mu'tazilite position fails. The dilemma shows their claim leads to a logical dead end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نلزمكم فنقول : لو كان العبد أوجده من حيث إنه فعل والله تعالى أوجده من حيث إنه قبح كما تزعمون للزم أحد أمرين كل واحد منهما محال. وبيانه أن العبد إذا قصد تحصيل الكفر فأوجده إما أن يكون الله تعالى مختارًا من إيجاده من حيث إنه قبح أو مضطر إلى ذلك؟ فإن قالوا: هو مختار نقول : كأن من الجائز أن يمتنع عن إيجاده من حيث إنه قبح فيوجد الكفر بدون صفة القبح وهو محال. وإن قالوا: هو مضطر في إيجاده فهو كفر لما فيه من دعوى المعجز على الله تعالى وإدخاله تحت قدرة الغير وقهره. ولا يلزمنا هذا في التخليق والانتساب على ما قررنا في شبهة الخصوم، لأن كل فعل في ذاته يوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على الانفراد والاستبداد، والعبد لا يوصف بذلك، إلا أن الله تعالى أجرى العادة أنه يخلق كل فعل قصد العبد اكتسابه. ونفرض الكلام في حركة معينة فنقول : هي قبيل وجودها علمها الله تعالى قدرة إيجادها لو أراد إيجادها على سبيل الاستبداد، والعبد لا يقدر على اكتسابها ومباشرتها بدون إيجاد الله تعالى. ولا يقال لو أراد الله إيجادها فعلاً للعبد مع امتناع العبد عن ذلك هل يحصل ذلك؟ فإن حصل فقد لزم القول باضطرار العبد إذ الفعل الضروري هو الذي يوجد والذي قصد العبد الامتناع عنه، وإن لم يحصل فقد لزم القول بتعجيز الباري جل وعلا، لأنا نقول : لا يتصور عندنا أن يريدها فعلاً للعبد إلا وأن يريد العبد وجود قدرته عليها لاستحالة وجود الفعل الاختياري بلا قدرة ولا يتصور وجود القدرة بدون الفعل فيستحيل إرادة الله إياها أن تكون فعلاً للعبد مع إرادة العبد الامتناع عنها إذ إرادة الامتناع لن تتصور بدون قدرة الامتناع، وقدرة الامتناع يستحيل وجودها بدون نفس الامتناع على ما مر في مسألة الاستطاعة.
Indeed, we constrain you by saying: If the servant brought it into existence from the perspective that it is an act, and God Almighty brought it into existence from the perspective that it is evil, as you claim, then one of two matters would be necessary, each of which is impossible. Its clarification: When the servant intends to acquire disbelief and brings it into existence, either God Almighty is free in bringing it into existence from the perspective that it is evil, or He is compelled to that. If they say: He is free, we say: It is as if it is possible for Him to refrain from bringing it into existence from the perspective that it is evil, so disbelief would exist without the attribute of evil, which is impossible. And if they say: He is compelled in bringing it into existence, then it is unbelief because it involves claiming incapacity for God and subjugating Him under the power of another. And this does not necessarily apply to us in the matter of creation and attribution, as we have established in the objection of the opponents, because every act in itself is described by God Almighty with power of independence and sovereignty, and the servant is not described with that, except that God Almighty has made it customary that He creates every act that the servant intends to acquire. And we consider discourse concerning a specific motion and say: Before its existence, God Almighty knew it with the power to bring it into existence if He wanted to bring it into existence in a sovereign manner, and the servant is not able to acquire it and perform it without God's bringing it into existence. And it should not be said: If God wanted to bring it into existence as an act of the servant while the servant refrains from it, would that occur? If it occurs, then the claim of compulsion of the servant would be necessary, for the compelled act is that which exists while the servant intended to refrain from it. And if it does not occur, then the claim of declaring the Creator incapable would be necessary. For we say: It is inconceivable in our view that He wants it as an act of the servant unless the servant wants the existence of his power over it, because it is impossible for a voluntary act to exist without power, and it is inconceivable for power to exist without the act, so it is impossible for God's will for it to be an act of the servant while the servant wills refraining from it, because the will to refrain cannot be conceived without the power of refraining, and the power of refraining is impossible to exist without the refraining itself, as has passed in the issue of capability.
Source pages p. 881

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on human action. The author presents a dilemma to the opponent who claims that the servant creates the act qua act, while God creates it qua evil. The dilemma: either God is free to create the evil quality or He is compelled. If free, He could refrain, so the act would exist without evil—impossible. If compelled, that implies God is under the power of another, which is kufr. The author then clarifies that this objection does not apply to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb), because God alone has sovereign power, and the servant's act is created by God according to custom. A specific example of a motion is analyzed: before existence, God knows it with power, but the servant cannot acquire it without God's creation. A further objection is addressed: if God wills the act but the servant wills to refrain, could it happen? If it does, the servant is compelled; if not, God is incapable. The answer is that such a scenario is inconceivable because a voluntary act requires the servant's power, and power cannot exist without the act, so God's willing it as the servant's act is incompatible with the servant's will to refrain, as established earlier.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
اضطرار
قبح
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's creation of evil is like human creation.
Correction: The dilemma shows that if God creates evil by necessity (compulsion), it implies imperfection.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if God is compelled to create the evil quality?
A) The act would exist without evil.
B) It is kufr because it claims God is incapable.
C) The servant would be compelled.
D) God's justice is upheld.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position collapses because it forces an impossible choice on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that every evil act you commit is directly created by God while you are merely the locus of the act, how would that change the way you feel about your own sins? Would you still repent with the same urgency, or would you feel less responsible?
?
The dilemma forces us to choose between God being free but allowing evil without its attribute, or God being compelled—both impossible. When you contemplate your own life, do you ever find yourself acting as if God were compelled to create your bad choices, thereby escaping accountability? How can you sincerely affirm that God is absolutely free yet still hold yourself fully responsible for your actions?
?
The Mu'tazilite position tries to protect God's justice by separating the act from its evil quality. But in your daily struggle with sin, do you ever separate your action from its moral weight—convincing yourself that ‘it was just my nature’ or ‘God made me do it’? How can you embrace the full weight of your choices without attributing evil to God?

The Ash'ari Doctrine of Kasb and Its Defense
Author defends Ash'ari kasb and analyzes a motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition) against the Mu'tazilite objection, and how God's sovereignty and human responsibility are harmonized.

Core Idea
God alone has sovereign power; the servant acquires the act created by God.

After presenting the dilemma against the Mu'tazila, the author clarifies why his own Ash'ari position is not subject to the same difficulty. He explains that God alone has independent power, and the servant's act is created by God according to custom, with the servant acquiring it. He then analyzes a specific motion to demonstrate the coherence of this view, addressing a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author transitions from refutation to defense of his own school.
This passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between God's independent power (istibdad) and the servant's acquired power. Find where this is stated.
3
Follow the analysis of a motion
The author gives an example of a specific motion. Trace the argument: before existence, God knows it with power, but the servant cannot acquire it without God's creation.
This shows the logical consistency of kasb.
4
Examine the objection and response
The author anticipates an objection about God willing the act while the servant refrains. See how he resolves it by linking will, power, and act.
The resolution relies on the impossibility of power without act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنا نلزمكم فنقول : لو كان العبد أوجده من حيث إنه فعل والله تعالى أوجده من حيث إنه قبح كما تزعمون للزم أحد أمرين كل واحد منهما محال. وبيانه أن العبد إذا قصد تحصيل الكفر فأوجده إما أن يكون الله تعالى مختارًا من إيجاده من حيث إنه قبح أو مضطر إلى ذلك؟ فإن قالوا: هو مختار نقول : كأن من الجائز أن يمتنع عن إيجاده من حيث إنه قبح فيوجد الكفر بدون صفة القبح وهو محال. وإن قالوا: هو مضطر في إيجاده فهو كفر لما فيه من دعوى المعجز على الله تعالى وإدخاله تحت قدرة الغير وقهره. ولا يلزمنا هذا في التخليق والانتساب على ما قررنا في شبهة الخصوم، لأن كل فعل في ذاته يوصف الله تعالى بالقدرة على الانفراد والاستبداد، والعبد لا يوصف بذلك، إلا أن الله تعالى أجرى العادة أنه يخلق كل فعل قصد العبد اكتسابه. ونفرض الكلام في حركة معينة فنقول : هي قبيل وجودها علمها الله تعالى قدرة إيجادها لو أراد إيجادها على سبيل الاستبداد، والعبد لا يقدر على اكتسابها ومباشرتها بدون إيجاد الله تعالى. ولا يقال لو أراد الله إيجادها فعلاً للعبد مع امتناع العبد عن ذلك هل يحصل ذلك؟ فإن حصل فقد لزم القول باضطرار العبد إذ الفعل الضروري هو الذي يوجد والذي قصد العبد الامتناع عنه، وإن لم يحصل فقد لزم القول بتعجيز الباري جل وعلا، لأنا نقول : لا يتصور عندنا أن يريدها فعلاً للعبد إلا وأن يريد العبد وجود قدرته عليها لاستحالة وجود الفعل الاختياري بلا قدرة ولا يتصور وجود القدرة بدون الفعل فيستحيل إرادة الله إياها أن تكون فعلاً للعبد مع إرادة العبد الامتناع عنها إذ إرادة الامتناع لن تتصور بدون قدرة الامتناع، وقدرة الامتناع يستحيل وجودها بدون نفس الامتناع على ما مر في مسألة الاستطاعة.
Indeed, we constrain you by saying: If the servant brought it into existence from the perspective that it is an act, and God Almighty brought it into existence from the perspective that it is evil, as you claim, then one of two matters would be necessary, each of which is impossible. Its clarification: When the servant intends to acquire disbelief and brings it into existence, either God Almighty is free in bringing it into existence from the perspective that it is evil, or He is compelled to that. If they say: He is free, we say: It is as if it is possible for Him to refrain from bringing it into existence from the perspective that it is evil, so disbelief would exist without the attribute of evil, which is impossible. And if they say: He is compelled in bringing it into existence, then it is unbelief because it involves claiming incapacity for God and subjugating Him under the power of another. And this does not necessarily apply to us in the matter of creation and attribution, as we have established in the objection of the opponents, because every act in itself is described by God Almighty with power of independence and sovereignty, and the servant is not described with that, except that God Almighty has made it customary that He creates every act that the servant intends to acquire. And we consider discourse concerning a specific motion and say: Before its existence, God Almighty knew it with the power to bring it into existence if He wanted to bring it into existence in a sovereign manner, and the servant is not able to acquire it and perform it without God's bringing it into existence. And it should not be said: If God wanted to bring it into existence as an act of the servant while the servant refrains from it, would that occur? If it occurs, then the claim of compulsion of the servant would be necessary, for the compelled act is that which exists while the servant intended to refrain from it. And if it does not occur, then the claim of declaring the Creator incapable would be necessary. For we say: It is inconceivable in our view that He wants it as an act of the servant unless the servant wants the existence of his power over it, because it is impossible for a voluntary act to exist without power, and it is inconceivable for power to exist without the act, so it is impossible for God's will for it to be an act of the servant while the servant wills refraining from it, because the will to refrain cannot be conceived without the power of refraining, and the power of refraining is impossible to exist without the refraining itself, as has passed in the issue of capability.
Source pages p. 881

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position on human action. The author presents a dilemma to the opponent who claims that the servant creates the act qua act, while God creates it qua evil. The dilemma: either God is free to create the evil quality or He is compelled. If free, He could refrain, so the act would exist without evil—impossible. If compelled, that implies God is under the power of another, which is kufr. The author then clarifies that this objection does not apply to the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb), because God alone has sovereign power, and the servant's act is created by God according to custom. A specific example of a motion is analyzed: before existence, God knows it with power, but the servant cannot acquire it without God's creation. A further objection is addressed: if God wills the act but the servant wills to refrain, could it happen? If it does, the servant is compelled; if not, God is incapable. The answer is that such a scenario is inconceivable because a voluntary act requires the servant's power, and power cannot exist without the act, so God's willing it as the servant's act is incompatible with the servant's will to refrain, as established earlier.

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
كسب
استبداد
اضطرار
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kasb implies the servant is compelled or that God creates evil acts directly.
Correction: Kasb means the servant acquires the act while God creates it; the servant's intention and power are involved, avoiding compulsion.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the scenario where God wills the act but the servant wills to refrain inconceivable?
A) Because God's will always overpowers the servant's will.
B) Because a voluntary act requires the servant's power, and power cannot exist without the act.
C) Because the servant cannot will to refrain from something God wills.
D) Because the act would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The servant's act is created by God and acquired by the servant; God's power is independent and the servant's power is dependent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God created that act and you acquired it by your own intention? How does this awareness change the sincerity of your repentance, or do you still feel that God is to blame?
?
The author argues that it is impossible for God to will an act from you while you will to refrain. Reflect on a time you felt 'forced' into a situation. Based on this lesson, can that feeling be accurate? What does this say about your own responsibility?
?
If every act of yours—even your good deeds—is created by God, what is left of your own effort? How does the concept of kasb challenge you to take your intentions more seriously and rely less on your own power?

Divine Decree
beginner
5 min
Human Acts as Accidents Known to God
Al-Nasafi states that human acts are accidents known to God before creation, with varying Mu'tazilite descriptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Nasafi argues that every human act is an accident known to God before its existence, and that the Mu'tazila differ on how to describe its pre-existent state.

Core Idea
Before existence, every human act is already known to God as a thing, accident, or motion.

Al-Nasafi begins a supporting argument for the createdness of human acts. He states that every human act is an accident (ʿaraḍ) and that it was known to God before it came into existence. He then notes that all theologians agree it is a 'thing' (shayʾ), but the Basrans call it a 'substitute' (ʿiwaḍ) and al-Jubbāʾī says that if it will be motion when found, it is motion even in non-existence. This shows that despite terminological differences, the act is an object of divine knowledge from eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice that al-Nasafi uses a supporting argument structure (''الذي يؤيد ما ذكرنا'').
The passage is dense with technical terms.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents three views on the pre-existent state of a human act. Find each: 1) all consider it a 'thing', 2) Basrans consider it a 'substitute', 3) al-Jubbāʾī considers it the same accident it will become (e.g., motion).
3
Understand the implication for divine knowledge
The point is that regardless of the label, God knows the act before it exists. This supports the author's position that human acts are fully within God’s knowledge and therefore created by Him.
This connects to the broader debate on qadar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يؤيد ما ذكرنا أن كل فعل من أفعال العبد عرض وهو قبل حدوثه وخروجه من العدم إلى الوجود كان معلوم الباري جل وعلا بلا خوف، وهو عندهم جميعًا شيء، وعند البصريين منهم عوض، وعند الجبائي إن كان عند الوجد حركة فهو في حال العدم أيضا حركة، ثم لا شك أن في حال عدمه معلوم الله تعالى، والله تعالى يعلمه شيئا في حال عدمه، وعندهم وعند البصريين يعلمه عرضا وعند الجبائي منهم يعلمه حركة. ثم الحال لا يخلو إما إن كانت لله تعالى عليه قدرة كما للعبد عليه قدرة ويصح من كل واحد منهما الانفراد بإيجاده كما هو مذهب طائفة منهم، وإما إن كانت قدرة الله تعالى غير متيقنة لما كان مقدورًا للعبد لاستحالة كون مقدور لقادرين كما هو مذهب جمهورهم، ولا وجه إلى هذا ؛ لأن الله تعالى هو الذي يتولى إعطاء العبد قدرة الفعل وهو المقدر له، ومن المحال أن يقدر ذات ما غيره على ما لا قدرة للمقدر عليه، ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن يقدر المقعد غيره على عين المشي الذي المقعد
And what supports what we have mentioned is that every action of the servant's actions is an accident (ʿaraḍ), and before its occurrence and its exit from non-existence to existence, it was known to the Exalted and High Creator without fear [uncertainty?]. And it is, according to all of them, a thing (shayʾ). According to the Basrans among them, it is a substitute (ʿiwaḍ). According to al-Jubbāʾī, if at the time of existence it is motion, then in the state of non-existence it is also motion. Then there is no doubt that in the state of its non-existence, it is an object of God's knowledge, and God knows it as a thing in the state of its non-existence. According to them and to the Basrans, He knows it as an accident; according to al-Jubbāʾī among them, He knows it as motion. Then the situation is not free from one of two possibilities: either God has power over it just as the servant has power over it, and it is valid for each of them to single-handedly bring it into existence, as is the doctrine of a group among them; or God's power is not certain over what is the object of the servant's power, because of the impossibility of one object of power belonging to two powerful agents, as is the doctrine of the majority of them. But there is no justification for this latter view, because God is the one who undertakes to give the servant the power of the act, and He is the one who empowers him for it. And it is impossible that one entity empower another over what the empowering one has no power over. If this were allowed, then it would be permissible for a disabled person [المقعد] to empower another over the very walking that the disabled person...
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage is a supporting argument for the author's position on divine power and human acts. Al-Nasafi states that every human act is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that exists after non-existence. Before its creation, it was known to God as a 'thing' (shayʾ) according to different schools: the Basrans (Muʿtazila) call it a substitute (ʿiwaḍ), and al-Jubbāʾī says if the act is motion when found, it is also motion in non-existence. All agree it is known to God. Then the author presents a dilemma: either both God and the servant have power over the same act, and either can create it individually (a view of some); or God's power over it is not certain because it is the servant's object of power, since one object cannot be under two agents (the majority view). Al-Nasafi rejects the second view because God is the one who gives the servant the power to act, and it is impossible for one to empower another over something he himself cannot do. If that were possible, a paralyzed person could empower someone to walk the very walking the paralyzed person does not have. The argument is incomplete in the provided text, but the intended refutation is clear: since God is the ultimate creator, human acts cannot be independent objects of power.

Key Terms
عرض
شيء
عوض
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that human acts are not known to God before they occur because they originate from human free will.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that all schools agree that acts are known to God before existence, affirming divine foreknowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, how do the Basran Mu'tazila describe a human act in its state of non-existence?
A) As a thing (shayʾ)
B) As a substitute (ʿiwaḍ)
C) As motion (ḥaraka)
D) As an accident (ʿaraḍ)

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of human acts before their existence is affirmed by all, even if they disagree on terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every sin you have ever committed was known to Allah as a specific accident before you were even born, what does that mean for your attempt to hide or justify it? Will you today repent with the certainty that nothing is hidden from Him?
?
The passage shows that even theological opponents agree on Allah's foreknowledge of your actions. How does this undeniable truth change the way you will approach a difficult choice you are facing right now?
?
Al-Jubbāʾī insisted that a motion is motion in God's knowledge before it exists. Do you treat your future deeds as already determined by your own plan, or do you submit them to Allah's knowledge and will? What specific step can you take today to align your intentions with His knowledge?
